<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Chromosome-scale assembly of the Kandelia obovata genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-05-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The mangrove Kandelia obovata (Rhizophoraceae) is an important coastal shelterbelt and landscape tree distributed in tropical and subtropical areas across East Asia and Southeast Asia. Herein, a chromosome-level reference genome of K. obovata based on PacBio, Illumina, and Hi-C data is reported. The high-quality assembled genome size is 177.99&#8201;Mb, with a contig N50 value of 5.74&#8201;Mb. A large number of contracted gene families and a small number of expanded gene families, as well as a small number of repeated sequences, may account for the small K. obovata genome. We found that K. obovata experienced two whole-genome polyploidization events: one whole-genome duplication shared with other Rhizophoreae and one shared with most eudicots (&#947; event). We confidently annotated 19,138 protein-coding genes in K. obovata and identified the MADS-box gene class and the RPW8 gene class, which might be related to flowering and resistance to powdery mildew in K. obovata and Rhizophora apiculata, respectively. The reference K. obovata genome described here will be very useful for further molecular elucidation of various traits, the breeding of this coastal shelterbelt species, and evolutionary studies with related taxa."/>

Herein, a chromosome-level reference genome of K. obovata based on PacBio, Illumina, and Hi-C data is reported.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The mangrove <i>Kandelia obovata</i> (Rhizophoraceae) is an important coastal shelterbelt and landscape tree distributed in tropical and subtropical areas across East Asia and Southeast Asia. Herein, a chromosome-level reference genome of <i>K. obovata</i> based on PacBio, Illumina, and Hi-C data is reported. The high-quality assembled genome size is 177.99âMb, with a contig N50 value of 5.74âMb. A large number of contracted gene families and a small number of expanded gene families, as well as a small number of repeated sequences, may account for the small <i>K. obovata</i> genome. We found that <i>K. obovata</i> experienced two whole-genome polyploidization events: one whole-genome duplication shared with other Rhizophoreae and one shared with most eudicots (Î³ event). We confidently annotated 19,138 protein-coding genes in <i>K. obovata</i> and identified the MADS-box gene class and the <i>RPW8</i> gene class, which might be related to flowering and resistance to powdery mildew in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>Rhizophora apiculata</i>, respectively. The reference <i>K. obovata</i> genome described here will be very useful for further molecular elucidation of various traits, the breeding of this coastal shelterbelt species, and evolutionary studies with related taxa.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Mangrove forests are coastal ecosystems with unique biodiversity that provides many ecosystem services and functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kauffman, J. B. et al. Shrimp ponds lead to massive loss of soil carbon and greenhouse gas emissions in northeastern Brazilian mangroves. Ecol. Evol. 8, 5530â5540 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2386e664">1</a></sup>. Mangrove loss will increase the threat of coastal hazards (i.e., erosion, storm surges, and tsunamis) to human safety and shoreline development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gilman, E. L., Ellison, J. C., Duke, N. C. &amp; Field, C. D. Threats to mangroves from climate change and adaptation options: a review. Aquat. Bot. 89, 237â250 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2386e668">2</a></sup>. Specifically, this will reduce coastal water quality and biodiversity and threaten adjacent coastal habitats, thereby weakening the main resources on which the human community relies, including a large number of products and services provided by mangroves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Nagelkerken, I. et al. The habitat function of mangroves for terrestrial and marine fauna: a review. Aquat. Bot. 89, 155â185 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2386e672">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Walters, B. B. et al. Ethnobiology, socio-economics and management of mangrove forests: a review. Aquat. Bot. 89, 220â236 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2386e675">4</a></sup>. Therefore, detailed studies and analyses of the genome and evolution of mangroves are urgently required, especially in the context of frequent human disturbance and inevitable sea-level rise.</p><p>The mangrove species <i>Kandelia obovata</i> belongs to Rhizophoraceae, which is called âQiuqieâ in Chinese, with the Latin name of <i>K. candel</i> in âFlora Reipublicae Popularis Sinicaeâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wight et al. in Flora Reipublicae Popularis Sinicae (ed Delectis florae Reipublicae Popularis Sinicae agenda academiae sinicae) Vol. 52, 133â135 (Sciences Press, Beijing, 1983)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2386e688">5</a></sup>. Later, in 2008, its Latin name was changed to <i>K. obovata</i> in the âFlora of Chinaâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Qin, H. N. &amp; David, E. B. in Flora of China (eds Wu, Z. Y., Peter, R.H. &amp; Hong, D.) Vol. 13, 295â299 (Sciences Press, Beijing, 2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2386e695">6</a></sup>. <i>K. obovata</i> is a woody plant predominantly found in tropical and subtropical tidal salt wetlands distributed from East Asia to Southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sheue, C. R., Liu, H. Y. &amp; Yong, J. W. H. Kandelia obovata (Rhizophoraceae), a new mangrove species from Asia. Taxon 52, 287â294 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2386e703">7</a></sup>. <i>K. obovata</i> adapts to transitional ecosystems where the land and ocean meet by overcoming periodic and aperiodic tidal effects, which induce high salinity, severe erosion, and anaerobic conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Giri, C. et al. Status and distribution of mangrove forests of the world using earth observation satellite data. Glob. Ecol. Biogeogr. 20, 154â159 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2386e710">8</a></sup>. <i>K. obovata</i> plays a crucial role in protecting biodiversity and combating erosion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Wardiatno, Y., Mardiansyah, Prartono, T. &amp; Tsuchiya, M. Possible food sources of macrozoobenthos in the manko mangrove ecosystem, Okinawa (Japan): a stable isotope analysis approach. Trop. Life Sci. Res. 26, 53â65 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2386e717">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhou, Q. et al. Characteristics and distribution of microplastics in the coastal mangrove sediments of China. Sci. Total Environ. 31, 134807 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2386e720">10</a></sup>. Specifically, the mangrove <i>K. obovata</i> can protect the embankment, accelerate the natural deposition of the beach, filter organic matter and pollutants from inland areas, and provide an ideal habitat for the marine flora and fauna<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Rogers, A. &amp; Mumby, P. J. Mangroves reduce the vulnerability of coral reef fisheries to habitat degradation. PLoS Biol. 17, e3000510 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2386e728">11</a></sup>. At the same time, due to its beautiful shape, unique floral pattern and fragrance, <i>K. obovata</i> is an excellent coastal wetland landscape plant and horticultural ornamental plant (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Morphological features of the flower and fruit of K. obovata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Morphological features of the flower and fruit of K. obovata.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="501" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> <i>K. obovata</i> trees in a coastal wetland. <b>b</b> Flowers. <b>c</b> Young fruits. <b>d</b> Cone-like fruits</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here, the genome of the mangrove <i>K. obovata</i> was sequenced using PacBio sequencing as well as the Illumina next-generation sequencing platform. These data can help clarify the history of mangrove colonization and mangrove adaptation mechanisms in intertidal zones. Furthermore, this study will provide a basis for the conservation of mangrove diversity and in-depth development of genetic resources for mangroves, as well as the development and utilization of coastal horticultural plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequence and assembly</h3><p><i>K. obovata</i> contains 36 chromosomes (2nâ=â2xâ=â36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Qin, H. N. &amp; David, E. B. in Flora of China (eds Wu, Z. Y., Peter, R.H. &amp; Hong, D.) Vol. 13, 295â299 (Sciences Press, Beijing, 2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2386e781">6</a></sup>. To assess genome size, survey sequencing was performed, and 65.27âGb of clean data was obtained (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The survey analysis indicated that the <i>K. obovata</i> genome size is 211.86âMb and has a low level of heterozygosity of approximately 0.38% (Supplementary Fig. 1). The assembled genome is 178.44âMb in size, with a scaffold N50 value of 279.55âkb obtained by using Illumina sequencing (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab1">1)</a>. To improve <i>K. obovata</i> assembly quality, we conducted Pacific Biosciences RSII sequencing and obtained 25âGb of single-molecule real-time long reads (average read length of 11.9âkb; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The final assembled genome is 177.99âMb in size, with a contig N50 value of 5.74âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab1">1</a>). The quality of the assembly was evaluated using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2386e807">12</a></sup>. The results showed that the gene set completeness of the assembled genome is 97.3%, indicating that the <i>K. obovata</i> genome assembly is very complete and of high quality (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab1">1</a>). Finally, high-throughput/resolution chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) technology was adopted to assess the chromosome-level diploid genome. The results showed that the lengths of the chromosomes ranged from 5.03 to 13.8âMb (Supplementary Table 2), with a total length of 178.01âMb and a scaffold N50 of 10.03âMb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig2">2</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The statistical results of Hi-C assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="612" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Intensity signal heat map of the Hi-C chromosome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>We confidently annotated 19,138 protein-coding genes in <i>K. obovata</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">3</a>), of which 19,136 (99.17%) were supported by de novo prediction, transcriptome data, and homolog prediction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">4</a>). The genome of <i>Rhizophora apiculata</i>, also belonging to Rhizophoreae, has 26,640 protein-coding genes, which is 7502 more than observed in <i>K. obovata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, S. H. et al. The origin, diversification and adaptation of a major mangrove clade (Rhizophoreae) revealed by whole-genome sequencing. Natl. Sci. Rev. 4, 721â734 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2386e950">13</a></sup>. The BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2386e954">12</a></sup> assessment indicated that the completeness of the gene set of the annotated genome was 90% for <i>K. obovata</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">5</a>). In addition, 105 microRNAs, 307 transfer RNAs, 167 ribosomal RNAs, and 199 small nuclear RNAs were identified in the <i>K. obovata</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Using homology-based and de novo approaches to identify transposable elements (TEs), we estimated that 24.07% of the <i>K. obovata</i> genome consists of repetitive sequences (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">8</a>) and 29% of the <i>R. apiculata</i> genome consists of repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, S. H. et al. The origin, diversification and adaptation of a major mangrove clade (Rhizophoreae) revealed by whole-genome sequencing. Natl. Sci. Rev. 4, 721â734 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2386e993">13</a></sup>. Compared with those of closely related nonmangrove plant genomes, the repetitive portions of the <i>R. apiculata</i> genome, comprising predominantly TE families, are significantly reduced, and the decrease in TE number largely resulted in a general decrease in genome size among true mangroves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, S. H. et al. The origin, diversification and adaptation of a major mangrove clade (Rhizophoreae) revealed by whole-genome sequencing. Natl. Sci. Rev. 4, 721â734 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1000">13</a></sup>. The small repetitive sequences may be one reason for the small genome of <i>K. obovata</i>. In addition, 18,266 genes were functionally annotated, among which 11,124 and 14,401 were annotated to Gene Ontology terms and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes terms, respectively, and 12,491 genes were functionally annotated in all five databases (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of gene families</h3><p>We constructed a phylogenetic tree and estimated the divergence times of <i>K. obovata</i> and nine other plant species based on genes extracted from a total of 1095 single-copy families (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">8</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">10</a>). As expected, <i>K. obovata</i> was sister to <i>R. apiculata</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">9</a>). The estimated Rhizophoreae divergence time was 83.15 Mya, and the divergence time between <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> was 24.63 Mya (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">9</a>). Next, using CAFÃ 3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1053">14</a></sup>), we found that 1110 gene families were expanded in the lineage leading to the Rhizophoreae, whereas 1368 families were contracted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig3">3</a>). Four hundred and ninety-five gene families were expanded in <i>K. obovata</i>, compared with the 1098 in <i>R. apiculata</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig3">3</a>). At the same time, 1604 gene families were contracted in <i>K. obovata</i>, compared with the 659 in <i>R. apiculata</i>. <i>K. obovata</i> has more contracted gene families than <i>R. apiculata</i> and fewer expanded gene families than <i>R. apiculata</i>, which may be the reason that the genome of <i>K. obovata</i> is smaller than that of <i>R. apiculata</i>. For the expanded gene families, we conducted GO enrichment analysis and found enrichment for the GO terms âstructural constituent of cytoskeletonâ and âstructural constituent of ribosomeâ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">11</a>). For the contracted gene families, enrichment was detected for the GO terms âprotein kinase activityâ, âterpene synthase activityâ, âoxidoreductase activityâ, ânutrient reservoir activityâ, âdefense responseâ, and âsulfotransferase activityâ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">12</a>). Gene families with <i>K. obovata</i>-specific expansion and contraction might relate to adaptation to <i>K. obovata</i>-specific coastal niches. Further research is required to validate the function of these genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The expansion and contraction of gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The expansion and contraction of gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="509" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The green number indicates the number of expanded gene families, and the red number indicates the number of contracted gene families. The blue color in the circle shows the gene families whose copy numbers are constant, while the orange color represents the proportion of 11,968 gene families in the most recent common ancestor that have expanded or contracted during late differentiation</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Synteny analysis and an ancient polyploidization event</h3><p>Whole-genome polyploidization events are a feature of many taxa and an efficient mechanisms of genome expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="McGrath, C. L. &amp; Lynch, M. Evolutionary significance of whole-genome duplication. in Poly-ploidy and Genome Evolution (eds Soltis, P. S. &amp; D. E., Soltis) 1â20 (Springer Berlin Heidelberg, Berlin, Heidelberg, 2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1121">15</a></sup>. To detect the occurrence of polyploidization events in Rhizophoreae, we used the default parameters of JCVI v0.9.14 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tang, H. B. et al. JCVI v0.9.14, &#xA;https://pypi.org/project/jcvi/&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1125">16</a></sup>) to analyze the protein sequences of <i>K. obovata</i>, <i>R. apiculata</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and obtained the gene pairs in the collinear regions. The results showed that there were 11,010 collinear gene pairs between <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>, 10,893 collinear gene pairs between <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>, 3,840 collinear gene pairs within <i>K. obovata</i> and 4,646 collinear gene pairs within <i>R. apiculata</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><p>We estimated the distributions of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i>s) values to more precisely infer the timing of polyploidization events in the <i>K. obovata</i> genome. The distributions of <i>K</i>s for paralogous <i>K. obovata</i> genes showed two peaks, one at <i>K</i>sâ=â0.38 and the other at <i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">10a</a>). The <i>K</i>s distribution of <i>R. apiculata</i> also had two peaks, one at <i>Ks</i>â=â0.32 and the other at <i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">10b</a>). The results suggested that <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> experienced two polyploidization events. To confirm these two polyploidization events, we further analyzed the <i>K</i>s distribution of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> and that of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. We observed that the <i>K</i>s distribution of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> had one peak, at <i>K</i>sâ=â0.1â0.16, which was smaller than the first peak in the <i>K</i>s distributions within <i>K. obovata</i> (<i>K</i>sâ=â0.38) and <i>R. apiculata</i> (<i>K</i>sâ=â0.32) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig4">4</a>). The first peak in the <i>K. obovata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â0.38) indicates that <i>K. obovata</i> shares a whole-genome duplication (WGD) event with other Rhizophoreae. In addition, we found that the <i>K</i>s distribution of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> had one peak, at <i>K</i>sâ=â0.9â1.4, which was also smaller than the second peak in the <i>K</i>s distributions within <i>K. obovata</i> (<i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9) and <i>R. apiculata</i> (<i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig4">4</a>). The second peak in the <i>K. obovata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9) indicates that the common ancestor of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> experienced an ancient polyploidization event. This event was shared by most eudicots, called the Î³ event, which is an ancient whole-genome triplication event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wu, S. D., Han, B. C. &amp; Jiao, Y. N. Genetic contribution of Paleopolyploidy to adaptive evolution in angiosperms. Mol. Plant 13, 59â71 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1316">17</a></sup>. Finally, we provide direct evidence of gene collinearity, as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig5">5</a>; the purple peak corresponds to the first peak of the <i>K. obovata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â0.38) and <i>R. apiculata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â0.32) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig5">5b, d</a>), and the green peak corresponds to the second peak of the <i>K. obovata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9) and <i>R. apiculata K</i>s distribution (<i>K</i>sâ=â1.5â1.9) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig5">5a, c</a>). The purple collinear region is an extra copy of the genomes of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>, and the green collinear region is also an extra copy of the genes in the genomes of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig5">5</a>). These copies correspond to two polyploidization events of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>. Therefore, our study verified that <i>K. obovata</i> experienced two polyploidization events: one WGD event shared with Rhizophoreae and one shared with most eudicots (Î³ event).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Ks distributions between K. obovata and R. apiculata and K. obovata and V. vinifera and within K. obovata and R. apiculata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Ks distributions between <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> and <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> and within <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>.</b></figcaption><div polyploidization events, and peaks of interspecies <i>K</i>s distributions indicate speciation events</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Collinear point diagram and Ks values corresponding to the collinear blocks."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Collinear point diagram and Ks values corresponding to the collinear blocks.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="856" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> The collinear point diagram of <i>K. obovata</i>. <b>b</b> Distribution of log10 (<i>K</i>s) values of the collinear blocks in <i>K. obovata</i>. <b>c</b> The collinear point diagram of <i>R. apiculata</i>. <b>d</b> Distribution of log10 (<i>K</i>s) values of the collinear blocks in <i>R. apiculata</i>. The ordinate of <b>b</b>, <b>d</b> is the number of gene pairs corresponding to the <i>K</i>s value, and the abscissa is the log10 (<i>K</i>s) value</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">MADS-box gene family analysis</h3><p>MADS-box genes play a key role in many important processes during plant development, especially during flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhang, L. et al. Genome-wide identification, characterization of the MADS-box gene family in Chinese jujube and their involvement in flower development. Sci. Rep. 7, 1025 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1476">18</a></sup>. We evaluated the MADS-box genes in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>. The <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> genomes encode 43 and 65 MADS-box genes, respectively. There are 12 type I and 31 type II MADS-box genes in the <i>K. obovata</i> genome and 31 type I and 34 type II genes in the <i>R. apiculata</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab2">2</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">14</a>). Interactions among type I MADS-box genes promote the initiation of endosperm development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Masiero, S., Colombo, L., Grini, P. E., Schnittger, A. &amp; Kater, M. M. The emerging importance of type I MADS box transcription factors for plant reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 865â872 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1505">19</a></sup>. The type I genes of <i>R. apiculata</i> were approximately three times more numerous than those of <i>K. obovata</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6a</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab2">2</a>). In addition, only 1 pseudogene type I genes were found in the <i>K. obovata</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">14</a>), suggesting that the type I MADS-box genes of <i>K. obovata</i> experienced a lower gain rate and higher loss rate than type II MADS-box genes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 MADS-box genes in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, <i>K. obovata</i>, and <i>R. apiculata</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of MADS-box genes from A. thaliana, O. sativa, P. equestris, K. obovata, and R. apiculata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Phylogenetic analysis of MADS-box genes from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>K. obovata</i>, and <i>R. apiculata</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc"
