/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree of type I MADS-box genes. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic tree of type II MADS-box genes. The number on the left in parentheses represents the homologous MADS genes of <i>K. obovata</i>, and the number on the right represents the homologous MADS genes of <i>R. apiculata</i>. The bolded gene ID numbers beginning with âKoâ represent the gene IDs of <i>K. obovata</i>; those beginning with âRaâ represent the gene IDs of <i>R. apiculata</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Type II MADS-box genes include two types: MIKC<sup>C</sup> and MIKC*<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Henschel, K. et al. Two ancient classes of MIKC-type MADS-box genes are present in the moss physcomitrella patens. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 801â804 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1781">20</a></sup>. MIKC*-type gene regulation has a major impact on pollen gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Adamczyk, B. J. &amp; Fernandez, D. E. MIKC* MADS domain heterodimers are required for pollen maturation and tube growth in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 149, 1713â1723 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1785">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Liu, Y. et al. Functional conservation of MIKC*-Type MADS box genes in Arabidopsis and rice pollen maturation. Plant Cell 25, 1288â1303 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1788">22</a></sup>. Plant MIKC<sup>C</sup>-type genes are the most widely studied MADS-box genes because they are essential for plant growth and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Theissen, G. &amp; Melzer, R. Molecular mechanisms underlying origin and diversification of the angiosperm flower. Ann. Bot. 100, 603â609 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1794">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, C. et al. Genome-wide characterization of the MADS-box gene family in radish (Rahpanus sativus L.) and assessment of its roles in flowering and floral organogenesis. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1797">24</a></sup>. The <i>K. obovata</i> genome has four MIKC*-type genes and 27 MIKC<sup>C</sup>-type genes, while the <i>R. apiculata</i> genome has three MIKC*-type genes and 31 MIKC<sup>C</sup>-type genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6b</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Tab2">2</a>). Fewer <i>C/D</i>-class and <i>AGL6</i> genes were found in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> than in rice, whereas more <i>B-AP3</i>-class and <i>E</i>-class genes were found in <i>K. obovata</i> than in rice (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6b</a>). <i>A</i>-class, <i>B</i>-class, <i>C/D</i>-class, and <i>E</i>-class gene clades are well known for their roles in the specification of floral organ identity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Sheng, X. G. et al. Genome wide analysis of MADS-box gene family in Brassica oleracea reveals conservation and variation in flower development. BMC Plant Biol. 19, 106 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1857">25</a></sup>, notably, the ABCDE flowering model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Coen, E. S. &amp; Meyerowita, E. M. The war of the whorls: genetic interactions controlling flower development. Nature 353, 31â37 (1991)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1861">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zahn, L. M., Feng, B. &amp; Ma, H. Beyond the ABC-model: regulation of floral homeotic genes. Adv. Bot. Res. 44, 163â207 (2006)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1861_1">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Silva, C. S. et al. Evolution of the plant reproduction master regulators LFY and the MADS transcription factors: the role of protein structure in the evolutionary development of the flower. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 1193 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1864">28</a></sup>. <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> have the same number of <i>A</i>-class and <i>B</i>-class genes (five members). <i>K. obovata</i> (six members) has more <i>E</i>-class genes than <i>R. apiculata</i> (four members), and <i>R. apiculata</i> (one member) has fewer <i>C</i>-class genes than <i>K. obovata</i> (three members) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6b</a>). The <i>AGL12</i> gene is involved in root cell differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature 498, 94â98 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1906">29</a></sup>, and the <i>ANR1</i> gene is involved in the regulation of lateral root development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhang, H. &amp; Forde, B. G. An Arabidopsis MADS box gene that controls nutrient-induced changes in root architecture. Science 279, 407â409 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1913">30</a></sup>. Furthermore, the loss of the <i>AGL12</i> gene may result in the loss of the ability to develop true roots for terrestrial growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature 498, 94â98 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1921">29</a></sup>. <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> each contain one <i>AGL12</i>-clade gene and one <i>ANR1</i>-clade gene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6b</a>), which may be because mangrove roots have adapted to environments at the interface of land and sea. <i>SOC1</i>, <i>SVP</i>, <i>FLC</i>, and <i>AGL15</i> regulate flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Searle, I. et al. The transition factor FLC confers a flowering response to vernalization by repressing meristem competence and systemic signaling in Arabidopsis. Genes Dev. 20, 898â912 (2006)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1953">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Reeves, P. A. et al. Evolution conservation of the FLOWERING LOCUS C mediated vernalization response: evidence from the sugar beet (Bsta vulgaris). Genetics 176, 295â307 (2007)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1953_1">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, J. H. et al. Role of SVP in the control of flowering time by ambient temperature in Arabidopsis. Genes Dev. 21, 397â402 (2007)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1953_2">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lee, J. &amp; Lee, I. Regulation and function of SOC1, a flowering pathway integrator. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 2247â2254 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1956">34</a></sup>. <i>SOC1</i> integrates multiple flowering signals related to photoperiod, temperature, hormones, and age<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lee, J. &amp; Lee, I. Regulation and function of SOC1, a flowering pathway integrator. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 2247â2254 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2386e1964">34</a></sup>. Notably, we found that <i>SOC1</i>-like genes were expanded in both <i>K. obovata</i> (five members of <i>SOC1</i>) and <i>R. apiculata</i> (seven members of <i>SOC1</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig6">6b</a>). Sequence variation among these <i>SOC1</i>-like genes could be associated with the functional diversification of the <i>SOC1</i> clade in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Disease resistance-related genes</h3><p>Plant resistance genes (<i>R</i> genes) exist in large families and usually contain a nucleotide-binding site (NBS) domain and a leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domain, denoted NLR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lozano, R., Hamblin, M. T., Prochnik, S. &amp; Jannink, J. L. Identification and distribution of the NBS-LRR gene family in the Cassava genome. BMC Genomics 16, 360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2010">35</a></sup>. According to the presence or absence of different domains in the N-terminal region, resistance genes encoding NBS domains can be divided into the TNL (TIR-NBS-LRR), CNL (CC-NBS-LRR), and RNL (RPW8-NBS-LRR) groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xiang, L. X. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of NBS-encoding genes in four Gossypium species. BMC Genomics 18, 292 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2014">36</a></sup>. A total of 165 and 292 nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-containing <i>R</i> genes were identified in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>, respectively; this might be because the distribution of <i>R. apiculata</i> is wider than that of <i>K. obovata</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig7">7</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Phylogenetic reconstruction of the NLR proteins in K. obovata and R. apiculata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Phylogenetic reconstruction of the NLR proteins in <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0300-x/MediaObjects/41438_2020_300_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="615" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>The NBS domain of human apoptotic protease-activating factor-1 (<i>APAF</i>-1) is located at the root of the tree. The bolded gene ID numbers beginning with âKoâ represent the gene IDs of <i>K. obovata</i>; those beginning with âRaâ represent the gene IDs of <i>R. apiculata</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We selected NLR candidate genes from <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> with complete domains to construct a phylogenetic tree. The results showed that these candidate genes were divided into the TNL, RNL, and CNL families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig7">7</a>). <i>RPW8</i> is a family of genes with highly specifically expressed characteristics, including resistance to powdery mildew<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Xiao, S. et al. The atypical resistance gene, RPW8, recruits components of basal defence for powdery mildew resistance in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 42, 95â110 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2079">37</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree showed that <i>RPW8</i> genes were significantly separated from all other CNL genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig7">7</a>). The <i>RPW8</i> clade contained two <i>K. obovata</i> and three <i>R. apiculata</i> genes and clustered with two <i>ADR1</i> genes from Arabidopsis, indicating that <i>RPW8</i> genes might be associated with resistance to powdery mildew (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#Fig7">7</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Although <i>K. obovata</i> is well known as a coastal shelterbelt and landscape tree in tropical and subtropical areas, research on this species has been hampered by a lack of genetic data. We obtained a chromosome-level reference genome of <i>K. obovata</i>, assembled a 177.99âMb genome, and annotated 19,136 protein-coding genes. A large number of contracted gene families and a small number of expanded gene families, as well as a small number of repeated sequences, resulted in a smaller genome in <i>K. obovata</i> than in <i>R. apiculata</i>. <i>K</i>s analysis revealed that <i>K. obovata</i> experienced two polyploidization events, namely, the recent WGD shared with other Rhizophoreae and the ancient polyploidization event shared with most eudicots (Î³ event). The Rhizophoreae divergence time was 83.15 Mya, and the divergence time between <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i> was 24.63 Mya. We identified MADS-box and <i>RPW8</i> genes in <i>K. obovata</i>, which might be related to flowering and resistance to powdery mildew, respectively. The genomic sequence analysis of the mangrove <i>K. obovata</i> helped reveal its mechanisms of adaptation to the intertidal zone; this knowledge is critical for understanding its genetic evolution and reproduction.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">DNA preparation and sequencing</h3><p>Fresh <i>K. obovata</i> tissues were collected from the Quanzhou Estuary Wetland Provincial Nature Reserve, Fujian Province, China. Genomic DNA was isolated from the fresh leaves of <i>K. obovata</i> for de novo sequencing and assembly. Paired-end libraries (500âbp) were constructed according to the Illumina protocol. Genome size and heterozygosity were measured using KmerFreq and GCE based on a 17-<i>K</i>-mer distribution. In addition, a 20âkb insert library was constructed according to the PacBio RSII protocol and subsequently sequenced on the PacBio platform (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#MOESM1">1</a>). The transcriptomes of different tissues of <i>K. obovata</i> were sequenced on the Illumina platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome assembly</h3><p>De novo assembly of the PacBio reads was performed. FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2191">38</a></sup> was used to correct errors in the original data. Then, SMARTdenovo v1.0 was used to assemble the corrected data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell. 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2195">39</a></sup>, and Arrow software (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>) was used to polish the assembly results. To further eliminate Indel and SNP errors in the assembly sequence, we compared the second-generation small-fragment data to the assembly results and corrected the assembly results again with Pilon v1.22 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2203">40</a></sup>). To confirm the quality of the genome assembly, we performed a BUSCO v3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2208">12</a></sup>) (<a href="http://busco.ezlab.org/">http://busco.ezlab.org/</a>) assessment using single-copy orthologous genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Hi-C library construction and assembly of the chromosome</h3><p>Fresh leaves of <i>K. obovata</i> were used to construct a Hi-C sequencing library, which was sequenced on the NovaSeq platform. SOAPnuke v1.5.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chen, Y. et al. SOAPnuke: a MapReduce acceleration-supported software for integrated quality control and preprocessing of high-throughput sequencing data. GigaScience 7, 120 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2227">41</a></sup>) was used to filter the original data (filtration parameter: filter -n 0.01 -l 20 -q 0.4 -d -M 3 -A 0.3 -Q 2 -i -G --seqType 1) to obtain clean reads. Then, the clean data were compared with the genome using Juicer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Sys 3, 95â98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2231">42</a></sup>. The results were filtered, and misaligned reads were removed. The genome sequence was preliminarily clustered, sequenced, and directed using 3D-DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92â95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2235">43</a></sup>. Juicer-box<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Sys 3, 95â98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2239">42</a></sup> was again used to adjust, reset, and cluster the genome sequence. Finally, we evaluated genome integrity using BUSCO v3 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2244">12</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Identification of repetitive sequences</h3><p>TEs contribute to genome dynamism in terms of both size and structure through insertions and eventual loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hawkins, J. S., Proulx, S. R., Rapp, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Rapid DNA loss as a counterbalance to genome expansion through retrotransposon proliferation in plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 17811â17816 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2256">44</a></sup>. Tandem Repeats Finder (<a href="http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>, v4.07) was used to predict tandem repeats across the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Benson, G. Tandem Repeats Finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2264">45</a></sup>. TEs were first identified using RepeatMasker v3.3.0 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) and RepeatProteinMask based on Repbase v21.12 (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase">http://www.girinst.org/repbase</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2277">46</a></sup>. Then, two de novo prediction software programs, RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, 351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2285">47</a></sup> and LTR_FINDER v1.06 (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhao, X. &amp; Hao, W. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2293">48</a></sup>, were used to identify TEs in the genomes. Finally, repeat sequences with identities â¥50% were grouped into the same classes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Homology-based, de novo, and transcriptome-based predictions were integrated to predict high-quality protein-coding genes. For homology-based prediction, homologous proteins from five available whole-genome sequences, namely, those of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, and <i>Salix purpurea</i>, were aligned to the <i>K. obovata</i> genome sequence using Exonerate v2.0 (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/psa/genewise/">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/psa/genewise/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2329">49</a></sup>. Gene structures were generated using GeneWise v2.4.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2333">50</a></sup>). Three ab initio prediction software programs, namely, Augustus v3.0.2 (<a href="http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/">http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2341">51</a></sup>, Fgenesh (<a href="https://omictools.com/fgenesh-tool">https://omictools.com/fgenesh-tool</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2350">52</a></sup>, and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2354">53</a></sup>, were employed for de novo gene prediction. Then, the homology-based and ab initio gene structures were merged into a nonredundant gene model using Maker v2.31.8 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2358">54</a></sup>). TopHat v2.0.11 was used to map RNA-seq reads to the assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2362">55</a></sup>, and Cufflinks v2.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq. experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2366">56</a></sup>) was applied to combine the mapping results for transcript structural predictions.</p><p>The protein sequences of the consensus gene set were aligned to seven protein databases, including GO (The Gene Ontology Consortium)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2373">57</a></sup>, KEGG (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29â34 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2381">58</a></sup>, InterPro (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/interpro/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2389">59</a></sup>, Swiss-Prot (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org">http://www.uniprot.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2398">60</a></sup>, and TrEMBL (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2406">60</a></sup>, for predicted gene annotation. The rRNAs were identified by aligning the rRNA template sequences from the Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2410">61</a></sup> database against the genome using the BLASTN algorithm with an E-value cutoff of 1Eâ5. The tRNAs were predicted using tRNAscan-SE v1.3.1 (<a href="http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/">http://lowelab.ucsc.edu/tRNAscan-SE/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2419">62</a></sup>, and other ncRNAs were predicted by Infernal software (<a href="http://infernal.janelia.org/">http://infernal.janelia.org/</a>) against the Rfam database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Genes from whole-genome sequences of ten species (<i>K. obovata</i>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Dimocarpus longan</i>, <i>Morus notabilis</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Rhizophora apiculata</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa</i>) were used for gene-family clustering analysis. OrthoMCL v2.0.9 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Fischer, S. et al. in Current Protocols in Bioinformatics (eds Andreas, D. et al.) Vol. 6, Ch. 6 (Zhang, 2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2466">63</a></sup>) was used to identify orthologous groups among the ten species. Pairwise similarities between all protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1Eâ5. To obtain reliable single-copy orthologous groups, we filtered out single-copy orthologous groups containing proteins of length &lt;200âbp. MUSCLE v3.8.31 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2474">64</a></sup>) was used to perform multisequence alignment of the protein sequences of the filtered single-copy orthologous group, and nucleotide alignment results were obtained by the corresponding relationship between protein sequences and nucleotide sequences. Finally, the nucleotide sequences of the single-copy orthologous group were connected to form a supergene, and then the data set was employed to construct a phylogenetic tree by using the GTRâ+âgamma model in MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540â552 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2478">65</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Estimation of divergence time</h3><p>The Markov chain Monte Carlo algorithm for Bayesian estimation was employed to infer the divergence time of each tree node using the MCMCTree module of PAML v4.7 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2490">66</a></sup>). The nucleic acid replacement model used was the GTR model, and the molecular clock model used was the independent rate model. The MCMC process included 100,000 burn-in iterations and 1,000,000 sampling iterations (with a sample taken every 100 iterations). To obtain a more stable result, the same parameter was executed twice. Calibration times were obtained from TimeTree (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene family expansion and contraction</h3><p>We measured the expansion and contraction of orthologous gene families using CAFÃ 3 (<a href="https://github.com/hahnlab/CAFE">https://github.com/hahnlab/CAFE</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2510">14</a></sup>. Based on maximum likelihood modeling of gene gain and loss, we analyzed gene families for signs of expansion or contraction using genomic data from the ten species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Collinearity analysis</h3><p>Within collinear segments, genes are conserved in function and sequence and remain highly conserved during the evolution of species. We used the default parameters of JCVI v0.9.14 (<a href="https://pypi.org/project/jcvi/">https://pypi.org/project/jcvi/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Rogers, A. &amp; Mumby, P. J. Mangroves reduce the vulnerability of coral reef fisheries to habitat degradation. PLoS Biol. 17, e3000510 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2526">11</a></sup> to analyze the protein sequences of <i>K. obovata</i>, <i>R. apiculata</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i> and obtained the gene pairs in collinear regions. Then, we used COGE (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/coge/">https://genomevolution.org/coge/</a>) for online analysis, examined the relationship between <i>K</i>s peaks and collinear regions, and verified the WGD event experienced by the common ancestor of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Whole-genome duplication</h3><p>We used <i>K</i>s distribution analysis to infer WGD events of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>. Diamond v0.9.24 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Benjamin, B., Chao, C. &amp; Daniel, H. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using diamond. Nat. Methods 12, 59â60 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2570">67</a></sup>) was used to conduct self-alignment of the protein sequences of the two species and then extract the mutual optimal alignment in the alignment results. Finally, Codeml in the PAML package was used to calculate the <i>K</i>s values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell. 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2578">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098â1103 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2581">68</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">MADS-box analysis</h3><p>The hidden Markov model (HMM) profile of the MADS-box gene family (PF00319) was obtained from Pfam (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org">http://pfam.xfam.org</a>). MADS-box gene family proteins were separately searched with HMMER 3.1 (with the default parameters)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2598">69</a></sup>. InterProScan v 5.19 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Finn, R. D. et al. InterPro in 2017âbeyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D190âD199 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2602">70</a></sup>) was used to identify MADS-box gene family candidates in the genomes of <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculata</i>. The genomic data of <i>R. apiculata</i> were downloaded from <a href="http://evolution.sysu.edu.cn/Sequences.html">http://evolution.sysu.edu.cn/Sequences.html</a>. MADS-box gene candidates were further confirmed with the 60 amino acid domains available from SMART<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Letunic, I., Doerks, T. &amp; Bork, P. SMART: recent updates, new developments and status in 2015. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D257âD260 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2620">71</a></sup> and online BLAST analysis (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>). Specifically, the protein sequence set for the MADS-box gene candidates was subjected to BLAST analysis against the assembled transcriptomes of the roots, stems, leaves, flowers, and fruits of <i>K. obovata</i> with the TBLASTN program. A phylogenetic tree was then constructed using MEGA5 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2631">72</a></sup>) with the default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Disease resistance genes</h3><p>Predicted proteins from the <i>K. obovata</i> and <i>R. apiculate</i> genomes were scanned using HMMER v3.1 (<i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2654">69</a></sup> using the HMM corresponding to the Pfam NLR protein family (NB-ARC: PF00931; TIR: PF01582; RPW8: PF05659; LRR: PF00560, PF07723, PF07725 and PF12799). To remove false-positive NB-ARC domain hits, InterProScan v5.19 was used to check the protein domains of the extracted sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Finn, R. D. et al. InterPro in 2017âbeyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D190âD199 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2659">70</a></sup>. The NBS domains of the genes confirmed by both HMMER and InterProScan were extracted according to InterProScan annotation and aligned using MAFFT v7.310 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Fischer, S. et al. in Current Protocols in Bioinformatics (eds Andreas, D. et al.) Vol. 6, Ch. 6 (Zhang, 2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2663">63</a></sup>); the alignment was then input into FastTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Price, M. N., Dehal, P. S. &amp; Arkin, A. P. FastTree 2-approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS ONE 10, e9490 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2667">73</a></sup> with the JTT model and visualized using EvolView<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="He, Z. et al. Evolviewv2: an online visualization and management tool for customized and annotated phylogenetic trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W236âW241 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2671">74</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Genome sequences have been submitted to the National Genomics Data Center (NGDC). PacBio whole-genome sequencing data and Illumina data have been deposited in BioProject/GSA (<a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa">https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa</a>.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Wang, Y. et al. GSA: Genome Sequence Archive. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 15, 14â18 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2718">75</a></sup> under accession codes PRJCA002330/CRA002395 and the whole-genome assembly and annotation data have been deposited in BioProject/GWH (<a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh">https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Database resources of the BIG Data Center in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D8âD14 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0300-x#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d2386e2726">76</a></sup> under accession codes PRJCA002330/GWHACBH00000000.</p>
